Interpreting Hearsay Testimony: The Rule and the Funeral Exception
Ruling on interpretation of hearsay testimony and the funeral exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a witness's interpretation that his testimony is based on hearsay renders it inadmissible, and identify the one exception for burial/funeral testimony.

Core Idea
If a witness explains his testimony is hearsay, it is rejected—except for burial/funeral prayer, which is treated as direct observation.

In Hanafi law, testimony must be based on direct knowledge (mu'ayana) or at least presented as certain. If a witness voluntarily reveals that his testimony rests on hearsay (tasmā') or merely seeing possession, the judge cannot accept it. However, there is a special rule for funeral matters: testifying to someone's burial or funeral prayer is considered direct even if the witness says he heard about it. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the witness's interpretation (tafsir) of the basis of his testimony. The ruling is that testimony becomes inadmissible if the witness says it is based on hearsay or seeing possession.
This applies even if the hearsay is widespread.
2
Locate the exception
Testimony about burial (دفن فلان) or funeral prayer (صلى على جنازته) is accepted even if the witness interprets it as hearsay. The text calls this 'direct' (معاينة).
The exception is specific to these two matters.
3
Explain the rationale
Such matters are typically known by public report; one cannot easily witness every burial. Thus, hearsay is treated as direct out of necessity and common practice.
This is a concession (rukhsa) to facilitate testimony on public events.
4
Apply the rule
Given a scenario where a witness interprets his testimony, determine whether it falls under the exception. If not, the testimony is rejected.
Always check: is the subject matter burial or funeral prayer?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن فسر للقاضي أنه يشهد بالتسامع أو بمعاينة اليد لا تقبل. ومن شهد أنه حضر أي الشاهد في موضع يجوز بالتسامع هذه الشهادة وهو الصحيح دفن فلان، أو صلّى على جنازته فهو معاينة حتى لو فسر للقاضي قبل. أي جنازة فلان أي الشاهد ذلك يسعه أن
And if he interprets for the judge that he testifies by hearsay or by seeing possession, it is not accepted. And he who testifies that he attended (the witness) in a place where hearsay is permissible, this testimony is correct: 'He buried so-and-so,' or 'He prayed over his funeral,' then it is direct, even if he interprets for the judge, it is accepted. Meaning 'the funeral of so-and-so', meaning the witness may do that.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of a witness's interpretation (tafsir). If a witness states that his testimony is based on hearsay or on merely seeing possession, the judge does not accept it because it reveals lack of direct knowledge. However, if the testimony concerns witnessing a burial or funeral prayer, that is considered direct observation (mu'ayana) even if the witness says he heard about it; such testimony is accepted. The key principle is that hearsay testimony must be presented as if it is certain, without specifying the source, to maintain the appearance of knowledge. The judge will not act on it if the witness explains it is based on hearsay, even if it is widely reported.

Key Terms
تفسير (tafsīr): Interpretation or explanation by the witness of the basis of his testimony (e.g., that it is by hearsay).
معاينة (mu‘āyana): Direct observation: testimony based on personal witnessing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Testimony based on hearsay is invalid unless it concerns burial or funeral prayer, which are treated as direct observation due to public knowledge.

Conditions for Accepting Testimony Based on Hearsay or Possession
Explains when testimony via hearsay or visible possession is accepted or rejected

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which testimony based on hearsay (tasammu') or visual possession is accepted or rejected in Hanafi fiqh, and identify the exception for slaves.

Core Idea
Testimony by hearsay is valid if the witness does not reveal its basis; explaining it invalidates the testimony.

In Islamic court procedure, the source of a witness's knowledge matters. This lesson from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies when testimony based on common hearsay or visible possession is legally sufficient, and when it must be rejected due to lack of direct knowledge. The Hanafi school balances the need to protect rights with the requirement of certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two bases of testimony
Recognize that testimony can be based on hearsay (tasammu') or direct observation of possession (mu'ayana al-yad). The ruling differs depending on whether the witness explains the source.
The term 'tasammu'' refers to widespread knowledge from public statements.
2
Understand the effect of tafsir (explanation)
If the witness explains to the judge that his testimony is based on hearsay or seeing possession, the testimony is rejected. If he does not explain, it is accepted.
This is the core ruling: the witness should not reveal the hearsay basis during testimony.
3
Examine the four scenarios of knowledge
The passage outlines four cases: (1) witness knows both owner and property by direct observation – allowed; (2) witness knows only by hearsay allowed if not explained; (3) witness heard about a property without seeing it – not allowed due to ignorance; (4) witness knows owner but not specific property – not allowed.
Scenarios 3 and 4 fail because knowledge of the subject matter is incomplete.
4
Analyze the slave exception
Seeing a slave in someone's hand is not proof of ownership because the slave has self-ownership. However, if the slave is a minor (cannot act for himself) or if his slavery is known, testimony is accepted.
Abu Hanifa imposed these conditions; the Sahibayn allowed absolute testimony for slaves seen in hand.
5
Note the practical advice for witnesses
A witness testifying by hearsay should not explain the source in court. He should simply state his testimony without mention of how he knows.
This preserves the validity of the testimony for the benefit of justice.

Source Text

كتاب الشهادة الشهادة وما يتعلق بها وإن فسر للقاضي أنه يشهد بالتسامع أو بمعاينة اليد لا تقبل. ومن شهد أنه حضر أي الشاهد في موضع يجوز بالتسامع هذه الشهادة وهو الصحيح دفن فلان، أو صلّى على جنازته فهو معاينة حتى لو فسر للقاضي قبل. أي جنازة فلان أي الشاهد ذلك يسعه أن وادعى ملكية هذا المحدود على شخص حل له أن يشهد استحساناً؛ لأن المالك يصير معلوماً بالتسامع، والملك بالمعاينة، ولو لم تسمع مثل هذه لضاعت حقوق الناس؛ والثالث: أن لا يعاينهما جميعاً، ولكن سمع من الناس أنهم قالوا: إن لفلان بن فلان ضيعة في بلدة كذا، حدودها كذا، وهو لا يعرف تلك الضيعة، ولم يعاين يده عليها، لا يحل له أن يشهد له بالملك؛ لجهالة المشهود به والمشهود له، والرابع: أن يعاين المالك دون الملك، بأن عرف الرجل معرفة تامة وسمع أن له في قرية كذا ضيعة وهو لا يعرف تلك الضيعة بعينها، لا يشهد؛ لأنه لم يحصل العلم بالمحدود، والمشهود به والعلم به شرط للشهادة. وقوله: "سوى الرقيق" يعني لا يجوز له أن يشهد في الرقيق إذا رآه في يد رجل أن الرقيق ملكه؛ لأن للرقيق يداً على نفسه، حتى إذا ادعى أنه الأصل كان القول قوله، ولا يمكن أن يعتبر فيه التصرف، وهو الاستخدام؛ لأن الحر يستخدم أيضاً طائعا كالعبد، وفي "الكافي" عن أبي يوسف ومحمد أنه يجوز له أن يشهد في الرقيق إذا رآه في يده، وفي "الهداية": جعل ذلك عن أبي حنيفة أيضاً لكن فيه قيدان آخران لم يذكرهما الشيخ لله، الأول: أن لا يكون الرقيق صغيراً؛ لأن الصغير الذي لا يعبر عن نفسه يكون في يد غيره؛ إذ لا يد له على نفسه فصار كسائر الأموال، فلو كان صغيراً يجوز له أن يشهد لمن هو في يده أنه ملكه، والثاني: أن لا يعرف رقه؛ لأنه إذا عرف رقه جازت الشهادة. [ رمز الحقائق: ١٤٤/٢] والحاصل أن مصرف الاستثناء إذا كان الرقيق كبيراً يعبر نفسه؛ لأن له يدا على نفسه فليس اليد في حقه دليل الملك (عيني، فتح) عن وإن فسر للقاضي: معنى التفسير أن يقولا: شهدنا؛ لأنا سمعنا من الناس، أما لو قالا: إنا لم نعاين ذلك، ولكنه اشتهر عندنا جازت في الكل، وقوله: "لا تقبل"؛ لأنه إذا أطلق يقع في قلب القاضي صدقه، فتكون شهادته عن علم، ولا كذلك إذا فسر وقال سمعت كذا وعن هذا كان المراسيل من الأخبار أقوى . من المسايند، ولهذا قالوا: ينبغي للشاهد بالتسامع أن يطلق أداء المشروع ولا يفسرها، وفي صورة التفسير لا يجوز للقاضي أن يحكم بها؛ ألا ترى أنه لا يجوز له أن يحكم بسماع نفسه، فكذا بسماع غيره ولو تواتر عنده، ولا برؤية نفسه في بد إنسان. (عيني، فتح) أو بمعاينة اليد : أي أو فسر للقاضي أنه يشهد بالملك برؤيته في يده. (عيني) فهو معاينة: أي حضور دفنه والصلاة على جنازته. (معدن) للقاضي: بأن قال: إني سمعت ذلك من الناس. (معدن) قبل تفسيره وتقبل شهادته؛ لأن ذلك يزيد القاضي علما، وهو لم يشهد إلا بما علم، فوجب قبولها. (عيني)
The Book of Testimony. Testimony and what pertains to it. If he explains to the judge that he testifies by hearsay or by seeing possession, it is not accepted. Whoever testifies that he attended the burial of so-and-so or prayed over his funeral - that is considered direct observation, so if he explains to the judge, it is accepted. ... (complete text would require full translation of all details)
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Testimony. It distinguishes between testimony based on hearsay (tasammu') and testimony based on direct observation (mu'ayana). The key ruling: if a witness simply testifies without mentioning the source, it is accepted. But if he explains that he only heard from people, the testimony is rejected, because the judge would then know it is secondhand. Four scenarios are given: (1) witness knows both the property and the owner by direct observation - testimony allowed. (2) witness knows only by hearsay - allowed if not explained. (3) witness only heard about a property without seeing it - not allowed due to ignorance. (4) witness knows the owner but not the property - not allowed. Then there is a discussion of slaves: since a slave has self-ownership, seeing a slave in someone's hand is not proof of ownership, except if the slave is a minor (who lacks independent capacity) or if the slave's status as slave is known. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow testimony for slaves seen in hand. The preferred view (mufta bihi) allows it with conditions. The passage also advises that witnesses should not explain the source when testifying by hearsay.

Key Terms
تسامع (tasammu'): Hearsay knowledge passed through common people's statements.
معاينة اليد (mu'ayana al-yad): Direct visual observation of possession.
تفسير (tafsir): Witness explaining to the judge that his testimony is based on hearsay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is testimony based on hearsay (تسامع) NOT accepted?
A) When the witness says 'I heard from people'
B) When the witness says 'I saw it in his hand'
C) When the witness says 'I attended his funeral'
D) When the witness is blind

Key Takeaway
Unreconstructed hearsay testimony is valid in Hanafi law, but revealing its hearsay basis destroys its legal force.

Who Is Not Qualified to Testify: Blind, Slave, Minor
Lists categories of rejected witnesses and exceptions, with a detailed blind testimony dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of persons whose testimony is rejected in Hanafi fiqh, identify the exception for slaves and minors, and explain the Hanafi position on blind testimony versus the opposing views.

Core Idea
Testimony of a blind, slave, or minor is rejected; only slave/minor who witness while in that state may testify after freedom/majority.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents the key rulings on who can testify in court. It lists three types of people whose testimony is not accepted: the blind, the slave, and the minor. However, there is an important exception for slaves and minors who witnessed an event while in that state and later testify after gaining freedom or reaching adulthood. The text also engages in a detailed legal dispute regarding the blind person's testimony, contrasting the Hanafi position with that of Zufar, Abu Yusuf, and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rejected categories
From the passage, list the three types of persons whose testimony is not accepted: the blind (al-a'ma), the slave (al-mamluk), and the minor (al-sabi). Note that these are distinct categories with different underlying reasons.
2
Understand the exception for slaves and minors
The text says 'except that they undertook the testimony in slavery or minority and testify after freedom and majority.' This means if a slave or minor witnessed an event while in that state, and then later when free/adult they give testimony, that testimony is accepted. This is because they were capable of bearing witness (tahammul) at the time, and the defect (slavery/minority) has been removed at the time of performance (ada').
This exception does not apply to the blind person; blindness is considered a permanent defect.
3
Analyze the blind testimony dispute
The passage presents the Hanafi position (with dalil) and the opposing positions. Identify the Hanafi argument: testimony requires pointing (ishara) to distinguish parties, which the blind cannot do. Contrast with Zufar (accepts in hearsay), Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i (accept if sight was present at observation). Note the Hanafi response that naming does not fully substitute for pointing.
This is a key example of ikhtilaf based on the sufficiency of alternative means of identification.
4
Extract the operative ruling
Despite the dispute, the mu'tamad (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school is that the testimony of a blind person is not accepted under any circumstances. The slave and minor testimony is accepted under the exception. Also note the four cases where a testimony previously rejected becomes accepted: slave freed, minor becomes adult, blind? Actually from the text: 'four: Abd, Sabi, A'ma, Kafir' – if the defect is removed, testimony accepted. For blind, if he becomes sighted? That is almost impossible, but theoretically.
The text mentions four categories including the blind and the disbeliever who becomes Muslim.

Chapter: Whose testimony is accepted and whose is not accepted. The testimony of the blind, the slave, and the minor is not accepted unless they undertook the testimony during slavery or minority (contrary to Zufar) and testify after freedom and majority. ... (full translation would cover all details including the four exceptions)

Commentary
This section begins a new chapter on who qualifies as a witness. It lists three categories whose testimony is not accepted: the blind person (al-a'ma), the slave (al-mamluk), and the minor (al-sabi). However, there is an exception: if a slave or minor witnessed an event while in slavery or minority, and later testifies after becoming free or adult, the testimony is accepted. This contradicts Zufar who accepted blind testimony in hearsay cases. The text then discusses the blind person in detail: the Hanafi position is that a blind person cannot testify even in hearsay because he cannot point to the plaintiff or defendant. Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i accepted testimony if the blind person had seen the event before becoming blind. The Hanafi response is that the ability to distinguish by voice is insufficient due to potential confusion. There is also a discussion about the slave and minor: their testimony is not accepted because they do not have authority over themselves. Other schools allow slave testimony. Finally, the text lists four cases where a testimony previously rejected due to a defect becomes accepted when the defect is rectified: slave becoming free, minor becoming adult, blind person? Actually the text says 'Abd, Sabi, A'ma, Kafir' - so the four: slave, minor, blind, and disbeliever (if they become Muslim).

Key Terms
الأعمى (al-a'ma): A person who is blind.
المملوك (al-mamluk): A slave (including qinn, mukatab, mudabbar, mu'ataq ba'd).
الصبي (al-sabi): A minor child.

Testimony of a Blind Person: Hanafi Position and Dispute
Ruling on blind witness with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the testimony of a blind person, explain the reasoning (inability to point), and compare it with the positions of Zufar, Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi‘i, and Malik.

Core Idea
A blind person cannot give testimony because he cannot reliably distinguish the parties by pointing.

The testimony of a blind person is a classic case where the Hanafi school insists on a physical condition (the ability to point) that other schools relax. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and the practical consequences for court proceedings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its reasoning
Identify the clear ruling: the testimony of a blind person is not accepted at all, whether he was blind at the time of hearing or later, and whether the matter is hearable or not. The key reason: performance requires distinguishing the parties by pointing, which the blind cannot do; tone of voice is uncertain.
The matn states the ruling; the commentary expands the scope.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their distinctions
List the differing views: Zufar accepts in hearing-based matters; Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i accept if the witness was sighted at the hearing; Malik accepts absolutely. Note the basis for each: reliance on hearing, the ability to have seen, or general qualification.
Opposing schools include Zufar (Hanafi internal), Abu Yusuf, al-Shafi'i, and Malik.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to each opposing argument
For Abu Yusuf's analogy to sighted witnesses who later go blind, the Hanafi response: pointing is essential; mentioning the name is insufficient when the person cannot be identified. For Zufar's emphasis on hearing, the Hanafi response: even in hearing-based matters, performance requires distinguishing the parties.
The Hanafi response is consistent: the condition of pointing is a prerequisite for valid performance.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: in Hanafi courts, blind testimony is never accepted. In Shafi'i courts, if the witness was sighted at the time of hearing, his testimony is accepted. In Maliki courts, it is accepted absolutely.
This affects the outcome of cases where the only witness is or becomes blind.
5
Note the mufta bihi and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the testimony of a blind person is not accepted in any case. Abu Yusuf's view (accepted if sighted at hearing) is a minority opinion within the school but not adopted for fatwa.
The tarjih authority is al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah, who prefers the majority view from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(فتح)] لما فرغ من بيان ما تسمع فيه الشهادة وما لا تسمع شرع في بيان من تسمع شهادته ومن لا تسمع منه، وقدم ذلك على هذا؛ لأنه محل الشهادة، والمحال شروط، والشرط مقدم على المشروط، والمراد من يجب قبول شهادته على القاضي ومن لا يجب، لا من يصح قبولها أو لا يصح. (عناية فتح ولا تقبل إلخ مطلقا سواء كان بصيرا وقت التحمل أو لا، وسواء كان فيما يجري فيه التسامع أو لا، وقال زفر : تقبل فيما يجري فيه التسامع وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة له، وبه قالت الثلاثة؛ لأن الحاجة في هذه الشهادة إلى سمعه، وهو صحيح لا آفة به، وعن مالك: تقبل شهادته مطلقاً كالبصير، وقال أبو يوسف إذا تحمل الشهادة في الديون أو في العقار، أو فيما لا يحتاج فيه إلى الإشارة إلى المدعي، ثم ذهب بصره فشهد قبلت شهادته وبه قال الشافعي؛ لأنه حصل له العلم بالمعاينة وقت التحمل، وهو في الحفظ والأداء كالبصير فلم يفته إلا الإشارة، وذكر الاسم يقوم مقامها عند تعذرها. ولنا: أن الأداء يفتقر إلى التمييز بين المشهود والمشهود عليه ولا يميز بينهما إلا بالنغمة، وفيه شبهة فلابد من الإشارة، وإنه يتعذر فيها، فلا تقبل. رمز الحقائق: ١٤٥/٢٢] ولو عمى بعد الأداء قبل القضاء يمتنع القضاء عند الطرفين وعند أبي يوسف لا يمتنع؛ لأنها أديت بشرائطها وقبلت فيقضى بها، كما لو مات الشاهد بعد الأداء أو غاب، ولهما أن قيام أهلية الشهادة شرط وقت القضاء؛ لأنها تصير حجة عنده، وقد فات، كما لو جن أو خرس أو فسق، بخلاف الموت؛ لأن الأهلية تستقر به ولا تبطل والشيء بانتهائه يتقرر والغيبة لا تنافي الأهلية. (فتح)
And the testimony of the blind person, the slave, and the minor is not accepted, except if they assumed the testimony in slavery and childhood, contrary to Zufar, and they perform after freedom and puberty. (That is, the slave and the minor, the testimony refers to the minor, the testimony in the slave, in the minor. Chapter of whose testimony is accepted, etc.: It is said: qabiltu al-qawl if you carry it upon truth. (Fath) When he finished explaining in what matters testimony is heard and in what it is not heard, he began to explain whose testimony is heard and whose is not heard, and he presented that before this because it is the place of testimony, and the place has conditions, and the condition is presented before the conditioned. What is meant is whose testimony is obligatory for the judge to accept and whose is not, not that it is valid or invalid. (Inayah, Fath) 'And it is not accepted' etc. absolutely, whether he was sighted at the time of assumption or not, and whether it is in matters where hearsay applies or not. Zufar said: It is accepted in matters where hearsay applies, and this is a narration from Abu Hanifa for him, and the three (imams) said the same because the need in this testimony is to his hearing, and it is sound with no defect. From Malik: His testimony is accepted absolutely like the sighted. Abu Yusuf said: If he assumed testimony in debts or in real estate, or in what does not require pointing to the claimant, then he lost his sight and testified, his testimony is accepted, and al-Shafi'i said the same because he acquired knowledge by visual observation at the time of assumption, and in preservation and performance he is like the sighted, so only pointing is missed, and mentioning the name takes its place when it is impossible. Our evidence: Performance requires distinguishing between the one testified for and the one testified against, and he cannot distinguish between them except by tone of voice, and there is uncertainty in that, so pointing is necessary, and it is impossible for him, so it is not accepted. Rumor al-Haqaiq 145/22. And if he became blind after performance but before judgment, judgment is prevented according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), and according to Abu Yusuf it is not prevented because it was performed with its conditions and accepted, so judgment is passed by it, just as if the witness died after performance or was absent. Their (the two) evidence: The qualification of witness competency is a condition at the time of judgment because it becomes a proof in his view, and it has been lost, like if he became insane or mute or immoral, unlike death because the competency is firmly established by it and does not become void, and a thing by its completion is determined, and absence does not negate competency. (Fath)
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the testimony of a blind person. The matn states categorically that the testimony of a blind person is not accepted. The commentary expands: The plain meaning is absolute, whether the person was blind at the time of hearing the testimony or became blind later, and whether the matter is one that can be heard or not. Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) and the three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Shafi'i according to one view) say that it is accepted in matters that rely on hearing, as the blind person's hearing is sound. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the person was sighted at the time of hearing and then became blind, his testimony is accepted for matters like debts or real estate where pointing to the claimant is not required. Malik accepts it absolutely. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is that performance requires distinguishing between the parties, which the blind cannot do reliably; pointing is necessary and impossible. They also discuss the case of blindness occurring after performance but before judgment: according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), judgment cannot be made because competency must exist at the time of judgment. Abu Yusuf disagrees, analogizing to death or absence. The Hanafi response distinguishes: death fixes competency, blindness destroys it.

Key Terms
الأعمى (al-a'ma): A person who is blind, i.e., lacks eyesight at the time of testimony.
التحمل (al-tahammul): The act of witnessing the incident (hearing/seeing) for later testimony.
الأداء (al-ada'): The act of giving testimony in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because he cannot hear the testimony properly.
B) Because he cannot distinguish the parties through pointing, and tone of voice is uncertain.
C) Because blind people are considered unreliable.
D) Because he may have forgotten the event.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects blind testimony due to the inability to point, a stricter condition than other madhhabs.

Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony of a Slave and a Minor: General Rule and Exception
Ruling on testimony of slave and minor with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state why the Hanafi school rejects the testimony of a slave and a minor, and identify the exception when re-testimony after a defect is removed is accepted.

Core Idea
Testimony requires guardianship; a slave or minor lacks it, so their testimony is rejected unless the condition is met at performance.

This passage establishes that the testimony of a slave (of any type) and a minor is not accepted in Hanafi law. The reasoning is that testimony is a form of guardianship (wilayah), and one who has no guardianship over themselves cannot have it over others. The passage then reports differing views from other schools and clarifies an important nuance: the condition of freedom and adulthood is evaluated at the time of testifying (ada'), not at the time of witnessing (tahammul). However, if a testimony was previously rejected due to a defect and then the defect is removed, a second testimony about the same event is accepted only in four specific cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the general ruling
The passage rules on the testimony of a slave (all types) and a minor. The general ruling: it is not accepted. Note that the mute, simple-minded, and insane (except when sane) are treated like minors.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is aqli: testimony requires wilayah (guardianship/authority). A slave and a minor lack wilayah over themselves, so they cannot have it over others. This is the Hanafi legal reasoning.
3
Note the ikhtilaf (scholarly difference)
The passage cites opposing views: Ahmad, al-Khuzaymi, Anas, Shurayh, Zurara, and others allowed slave testimony in some cases; Malik allowed minor testimony in matters only minors witness. This situates the Hanafi position in the broader ikhtilaf.
4
Understand the condition of time (ada' vs tahammul)
The passage states 'the consideration is the time of performance.' This means if a slave is freed, or a minor reaches puberty, before testifying, they become qualified. Their earlier incapacity at the time of witnessing does not invalidate their testimony.
This is a key Hanafi principle: condition evaluated at ada'.
5
Identify the exception for re-testimony after defect removal
If a testimony was rejected due to a defect (e.g., slavery, minority, blindness, disbelief) and then the defect is removed, a second testimony about the same event is not accepted except in four cases: slave, minor, blind, and disbeliever. For these four, the second testimony is accepted.
This exception is specific and limited; for other defects, re-testimony after removal is not accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمملوك والصبي: أي المملوك مطلقاً سواء كان قناً أو مكاتباً أو مدبراً أو معتق البعض، وحكم الأخرس والمغفل كالصبي، وكذا المجنون إلا في حال الصحة، وإنما لا تقبل شهادة المملوك والصبي؛ لأنها من باب الولاية، وليس ولاية على أنفسهما فلا تكون على غيرهما، وعن أحمد يجوز شهادة المملوك، وعن الخزفي: تجوز شهادة العبد في كل شيء إلا في الحدود وقال البخاري في "صحيحه": وقال أنس: شهادة العبد جائزة إذا كان عدلاً، وأجازه شریح وزرارة بن أبي أوفى، وعند مالك: شهادة الصبي فيما لا يحضره إلا الصبيان ،تقبل، وقال ابن سيرين: شهادة العبد جائزة إلا لسيده وأجازه الحسن وإبراهيم في الشيء التافه. (عيني، فتح) وأديا بعد إلخ لأنهما أهل للتحمل وكذا بعد الصباء وإسلام وتوبة فسق وطلاق زوجة؛ لأن المعتبر حال الأداء، ومتى حكم برد الشهادة لعلة ثم زالت فشهد فيها لم تقبل إلا في أربعة عبد وصبي وأعمى وكافر =
And the slave and the minor: meaning the slave absolutely, whether he is a full slave, a mukatab, a mudabbar, or partially freed. And the ruling of the mute and the simple-minded is like the minor, and similarly the insane except in a healthy state. The testimony of the slave and minor is not accepted because it is from the category of guardianship (wilayah), and they have no guardianship over themselves, so they cannot have it over others. From Ahmad: the testimony of the slave is permissible. From al-Khuzaymi: the testimony of the slave is permissible in everything except hadd punishments. Al-Bukhari said in his Sahih: Anas said: the testimony of a slave is permissible if he is just. Shurayh and Zurara ibn Abi Awfa permitted it. According to Malik: the testimony of a minor is accepted in matters that only minors are present for. Ibn Sirin said: the testimony of a slave is permissible except against his master, and al-Hasan and Ibrahim permitted it for trivial things. (Ayni, Fath) 'And they perform after' etc. because they are qualified for bearing testimony, and similarly after childhood, Islam, repentance from sin, divorce of a wife, because the consideration is the time of performance. And whenever a testimony is rejected due to a defect and then the defect is removed, then he testifies in it, it is not accepted except in four: slave, minor, blind, and disbeliever =
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with supporting proofs. It first defines the scope: 'slave' includes all types of slaves; the mute and simple-minded are like minors; the insane only when sane. The reasoning is that testimony requires guardianship, and a slave or minor lacks guardianship even over himself. The commentary then reports opposing views: Ahmad, al-Khuzaymi, some Companions and Successors allowed slave testimony in some cases; Malik allowed minor testimony in minor-specific matters. The Hanafi position is that the condition of freedom and adulthood is evaluated at the time of testimony (ada'), not at the time of witnessing (tahammul). However, if a testimony was rejected due to a defect (e.g., slavery) and later the defect is removed (e.g., the slave is freed), and the person testifies again, it is not accepted except in four cases: slave, minor, blind, and disbeliever. This exception means if these four people bore testimony while defective, and then become qualified (free, adult, sighted, Muslim) and later testify about the same event, their testimony is accepted. The '=' at the end likely indicates the end of a section in the manuscript.

Key Terms
المملوك (al-mamluk): A slave, including qinn, mukatab, mudabbar, and mu'ataq ba'd
الولاية (al-wilayah): Legal authority or capacity to act on behalf of others
الصبي (al-sabi): A minor who has not reached puberty
الأداء (al-ada'): The time of performing testimony in court
التحمل (al-tahammul): The time of bearing/witnessing the event

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as stated in this passage, when a testimony is rejected due to a defect (e.g., slavery) and then the defect is removed, in how many cases is a second testimony about the same event accepted?
A) In all cases
B) In five cases
C) In four cases
D) In none

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects testimony of slaves and minors due to lack of wilayah, but if the defect is removed before testifying, it becomes acceptable; a second testimony after a prior rejection is only accepted in four specific cases.

Testimony of the One Flogged for Qadhf: A Perpetual Disqualification?
Ruling on testimony of one flogged for false accusation even after repentance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the testimony of one flogged for qadhf is never accepted even after repentance, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using Quran 2:160.

Core Idea
The word 'abadan' in Quran 24:4 makes the disqualification of a qadhf-flogged witness eternal, beyond repentance.

This passage addresses a sharp inter-madhhab disagreement: can a person who was flogged for falsely accusing someone of zina ever serve as a witness again? The Hanafi school insists on a permanent ban, while the Shafi'i school allows it after sincere repentance. The debate hinges on the precise referent of pronouns and the nature of an exception clause in the Quran. We will dissect both proofs and understand why the Hanafi position is considered textually conclusive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is giving testimony after having been flogged for qadhf. The Hanafi ruling: such testimony is not accepted even after repentance.
There is no intra-Hanafi conflict; the three imams agree.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof from Quran 24:4
The verse commands: 'And never accept their testimony forever'. The word 'abadan' (forever) is decisive in indicating perpetuity.
The Dalil Block in the toolbox cites the full verse.
3
Examine the opposing proof from Quran 2:160
Al-Shafi'i uses 'Except those who repent' to argue that repentance lifts all legal consequences, including disqualification from testimony.
This is a general principle applied to the specific case.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response: disconnected exception and pronoun reference
The Hanafi response is twofold: (a) the exception 'except those who repent' in 24:4 applies not to the flogged but to the description 'transgressors', making it a disconnected exception; (b) the pronoun 'them' in 24:4 refers specifically to the flogged, not to all accusers.
The commentary explains the linguistic argument using the waw isti'nafiyyah.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi courts, a qadhf-flogged person can never testify; in Shafi'i courts, his testimony is accepted after repentance.
This affects real cases of credibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني، فتح) والمحدود في قذف إن لم تقم بينة على صدقه وكل فاسق تاب عن فسقه قبلت توبته وشهادته إلا اثنين: المحدود في قذف والمعروف بالكذب، فتوبتهما مقبولة عند الله تعالى، لكن لا تقبل شهادتهما، وقال الشافعي: تقبل شهادة المحدود في قذف إذا تاب؛ لقوله تعالى: إِلَّا الَّذِينَ تَابُوالله (البقرة: ١٦٠)؛ إذ حكم المستثنى بخلاف المستثنى منه، وبه قال مالك وعثمان البتي ولنا: قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقْبَلُوا لَهُمْ شَهَادَةً أَبَدا (النور:٤)، والمستثنى صفة الفسق في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْفَاسِقُونَ (النور:٤)، والواو فيه للاستئناف لا للعطف، فيكون منقطعاً عن الأولى فينصرف الاستثناء إليه ضرورة. [رمز الحقائق: ١٤٥/٢] وأن أولئك هم منه فإن قيل: كيف ينصرف الاستثناء إلى ما يليه والأصل في الاستثناء هو المتصل، وهو أن يكون المستثنى من جنس المستثنى منه، والتوبة ليست من جنس الفسق، قلنا: الاستثناء من عموم الأحوال كأنه قال: - والله أعلم الفاسقون في جميع الأحوال إلا في حال التوبة، فيكون الاستثناء متصلا، أو نقول: الاستثناء منقطع بمعنى "لكن"، كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّهُمْ عَدُوٌّ لِي إِلَّا رَبَّ الْعَالَمِينَ ﴾ (الشعراء: ۷۷) فلا . يرد السؤال. واعلم الضمير في "لهم" من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقْبَلُوا لَهُمْ شَهَادَةً أَبَدا (النور:٤) عائد إلى المحدودين، وعند الشافعي إلى القاذفين العاجزين عن الإثبات، فلو لم يحد تقبل شهادته عندنا خلافاً له، ولو أقام أربعة بعد ما حد على أنه زنى تقبل شهادته بعد التوبة في الصحيح؛ لأنه بعد إقامة البينة لا يحد، فكذا لا ترد شهادته، وفي المحدود في غير القذف تقبل شهادته إن تاب. (فتح) وإن تاب: أي عن جناية القذف وهذا عندنا. (معدن)
And the one flogged for qadhf (false accusation of zina), even if he repents, except if the disbeliever is flogged for qadhf then converts, and the child for his parents, upon a Muslim. And upon this, the testimony of a husband after it is rejected is not accepted, even after divorce. What is meant by 'after divorce' is the expiration of the waiting period ('iddah), and during the 'iddah it is not accepted. (Ayni, Fath). And the one flogged for qadhf, if he does not establish evidence of his truthfulness, and every sinner who repents from his sin, his repentance and testimony are accepted except two: the one flogged for qadhf and the known liar. Their repentance is accepted with Allah, but their testimony is not accepted. Al-Shafi'i said: the testimony of the one flogged for qadhf is accepted if he repents, because of His statement: 'Except those who repent' (Quran 2:160), since the exception is from the general rule. Malik and Uthman al-Batti also said that. Our evidence: His statement: 'And never accept their testimony' (Quran 24:4), and the excepted is the description of transgression in His statement: 'And those are the transgressors' (Quran 24:4), and the waw is for resumption not conjunction, so it is disconnected from the first, so the exception necessarily applies to it. Rumor al-Haqaiq 145/2. 'And those are from it.' If it is said: How can the exception apply to what follows it, while the principle in exception is that it is connected, meaning the excepted must be of the same kind as the thing excepted from, and repentance is not of the same kind as transgression? We say: The exception is from the generality of states, as if He said: 'and Allah knows best the transgressors in all states except in the state of repentance,' so the exception is connected. Or we say: the exception is disconnected meaning 'but', like His statement: 'Indeed they are an enemy to me, except the Lord of the worlds' (Quran 26:77) so the question does not arise. And know that the pronoun 'them' in His statement 'And never accept their testimony' (Quran 24:4) refers to the flogged ones, and according to al-Shafi'i, it refers to the accusers who are unable to prove. So if he is not flogged, his testimony is accepted according to us, contrary to him. And if he brings four witnesses after he was flogged that he committed zina, his testimony is accepted after repentance in the correct view, because after establishing evidence he is not flogged, so his testimony is not rejected. And in the one flogged for something other than qadhf, his testimony is accepted if he repents. (Fath) 'And he repents': meaning from the crime of qadhf, and this is according to us. (Ma'din)
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on whether the testimony of a person flogged for qadhf (false accusation of zina) is accepted after repentance. The matn states that it is not accepted even if he repents, with an exception for a disbeliever flogged for qadhf who later converts to Islam. The commentary clarifies: every sinner who repents has his testimony accepted except two categories: the one flogged for qadhf and the known liar. Their repentance is accepted in the sight of Allah but not for legal testimony. Al-Shafi'i argues that the verse 'Except those who repent' (Quran 2:160) creates an exception for the flogged if he repents. The Hanafi response cites Quran 24:4 'And never accept their testimony' which is eternal ('abadan), and argues that the exception in verse 24:4 applies to the description of 'transgressors' not to the flogged themselves, making the exception disconnected. They also analyze the pronoun 'them' in 24:4: Hanafis say it refers to the flogged ones, Shafi'i says it refers to the accusers who fail to prove. The passage also notes: if the flogging is for a non-qadhf crime, testimony is accepted after repentance. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that the testimony of the qadhf-flogged is never accepted, even if he repents.

Key Terms
المحدود في قذف (al-mahdud fi qadhf): A person who has been flogged with eighty lashes for falsely accusing a chaste person of zina without producing four witnesses
التوبة (al-tawbah): Repentance from sin, which includes regret, ceasing, and resolve not to return
أبدا (abadan): Forever, perpetually

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the person is considered a liar by nature.
D) Because the punishment must be repeated if he tries to testify.

Key Takeaway
The testimony of a person flogged for qadhf is permanently rejected in the Hanafi school, even if he repents, due to the explicit perpetual prohibition in Quran 24:4.

Testimony of a Dhimmi Flogged for Qadhf Who Converts
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partial flogging

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a dhimmi flogged for qadhf after conversion and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding partial flogging.

Core Idea
Conversion creates a new testimony capacity, but the timing of the flogging determines acceptance.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a nuanced situation: a non-Muslim who is flogged for false accusation (qadhf) converts to Islam thereafter. The question is whether his testimony is accepted. The Hanafi school provides a detailed answer, distinguishing between completion of the hadd punishment and the emergence of a new legal capacity. We will analyze the ruling, its rationale, and the internal disagreement among Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The passage states that if a disbeliever is flogged for qadhf then converts, his testimony is accepted. The rationale: rejection of testimony is part of the complete hadd, and conversion grants a fresh capacity.
2
Contrast with the slave case
A slave flogged for qadhf then freed does not have his testimony accepted because at the time of flogging he had no testimony capacity. The rejection was only completed after emancipation.
3
Apply the partial flogging scenario
If the flogging is interrupted by conversion, the majority view says testimony is accepted as long as flogging is not completed before conversion.
4
Explain Abu Hanifa's narrations
Abu Hanifa has two views: (a) if the last lash is after Islam, not accepted; (b) if the majority (akthar) of lashes are after Islam, not accepted; otherwise accepted.
The principle: 'al-akthar yanzilu manzilat al-kull' (the majority takes the ruling of the whole).
5
Conclude with the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the majority view, as per Al-Marghinani and others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا أن يحد الكافر في قذف، ثم أسلم، والولد لأبويه، على مسلم، وعلى هذا لا تقبل شهادة الزوج بعد ردها ولو بعد الطلاق، فالمراد بما بعد الطلاق انقضاء العدة، وفي العدة لا تقبل. (عيني، فتح) إلا أن يحد الكافر إلخ تقبل شهادته بعد الإسلام؛ لأن للكافر شهادة على مثله، له ذلك ومن وحد في القذف، كان رد شهادته من تتمة حد وبالإسلام حدثت له شهادة مطلقة غير الأولى، فلا يكون الرد من تمامها، بخلاف العبد إذا حد ثم أعتق حيث لم تقبل شهادته؛ لأنه لم يكن له شهادة على أحد وقت الجلد فلم يتم الرد إلا بعد الإعتاق، ولو ضرب الذمي في حد القذف سوطاً، ثم أسلم، ثم ضرب الباقي بعد الإسلام تقبل شهادته وعن أبي حنيفة إذا ضرب السوط الأخير بعد الإسلام، لا تقبل، وعنه: أنه إذا ضرب الأكثر بعد الإسلام لا تقبل شهادته، وإن كان دون ذلك تقبل؛ لأن للأكثر حكم الكل. رمز الحقائق: ١٤٦/٢] (عناية)
Except if the disbeliever is flogged for qadhf, then converts, and the child for his parents, upon a Muslim. And upon this, the testimony of a husband after it is rejected is not accepted, even after divorce. What is meant by 'after divorce' is the expiration of the waiting period ('iddah), and during the 'iddah it is not accepted. (Ayni, Fath) Except if the disbeliever is flogged, etc. His testimony is accepted after Islam because the disbeliever has testimony over his like. He has that. And whoever is flogged for qadhf, the rejection of his testimony is part of the completion of the hadd punishment, and by Islam a new absolute testimony occurs for him that is not the first, so the rejection is not part of its completion. Unlike a slave if he is flogged then freed, his testimony is not accepted because he had no testimony over anyone at the time of the flogging, so the rejection was not completed except after emancipation. And if a dhimmi is struck one lash in the hadd of qadhf, then converts, then the remainder is struck after Islam, his testimony is accepted. And from Abu Hanifa: if the last lash is struck after Islam, it is not accepted. And from him: if the majority is struck after Islam, his testimony is not accepted, but if it is less than that, it is accepted, because the majority takes the ruling of the whole. Rumor al-Haqaiq 146/2. (Inayah)
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the exception for a disbeliever flogged for qadhf who converts to Islam. The matn states that if a disbeliever is flogged for qadhf and then converts, his testimony is accepted. The commentary explains the rationale: a disbeliever has no prior valid testimony that would be rejected as part of the hadd; upon conversion, he acquires a completely new capacity for testimony unaffected by the earlier flogging. This is contrasted with a slave who is flogged for qadhf then freed: his testimony remains rejected because at the time of flogging, he had no testimony capacity, so the rejection is fully completed only after emancipation. The passage then details a scenario where the flogging is partially carried out before conversion. According to the majority view, if he converts before the flogging is completed, his testimony is accepted. Abu Hanifa has two narrations: one says if the last lash is after Islam, it is not accepted; another says if the majority of lashes are after Islam, it is not accepted; otherwise it is accepted. The principle is that the majority takes the ruling of the whole. This shows a nuanced application of the rule that the rejection of testimony is tied to the completion of the hadd punishment.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state
تتمة حد (tatimat hadd): Completion of the hadd punishment; the rejection of testimony is part of the complete hadd for qadhf
أكثر (akthar): Majority; used to determine when a partial flogging counts as complete

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His testimony is not accepted because the flogging was interrupted
B) His testimony is accepted because the flogging was not completed before conversion
C) His testimony is accepted only if he repents
D) His testimony is not accepted because the majority of lashes (40 out of 80) equals the whole

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi flogged for qadhf who converts before the punishment is complete may have his testimony accepted, due to the creation of a new legal capacity, but the exact timing of the lashes creates an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Testimony of Child for Parents and Close Relatives
Ruling on testimony of a child for his parents and related prohibitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony between close relatives and explain the rationale based on the hadith and the principle of connected benefits (al-manafi' al-muttasilah).

Core Idea
Testimony between close relatives is rejected because of interconnected benefits.

This passage addresses a critical area of shahadah (testimony): when family ties or dependency create an inherent conflict of interest. The author cites a hadith from Ali (ra) listing seven prohibited relationships and explains the underlying rationale: the benefits between these individuals are so connected that testifying for one is like testifying for oneself. Understanding this ruling helps a jurist assess the validity of testimonies in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states: 'The child for his parents' – meaning the testimony of a child in favor of his parents is not accepted. The author then lists additional prohibited relationships.
The ruling is explicit: such testimonies are rejected.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is a hadith attributed to Ali (ra) which enumerates seven categories where testimony is not accepted. The hadith is quoted verbatim.
The hadith is ahad (single-transmitter), so its thubut is zanni, but the Hanafi school relies on it.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit prohibition ('la tuqbal') directly indicates that testimony in these relationships is invalid. The rationale is given: 'because benefits are connected' (li-anna al-manafi' muttasilah).
The ruling is clear from the text; no further inference needed.
4
Appreciate the scope
The hadith covers: child for parent, parent for child, wife for husband, husband for wife, slave for master, master for slave, hired worker for hirer. The commentator suggests a more general wording ('branch for root', 'ancestors') would be even better.
Siblings, cousins, and in-laws are not included in this prohibition.
5
Note the rationale and ikhtilaf
The rationale is interconnected benefits (manafi' muttasilah), creating suspicion of partiality. This ruling is mukhtalaf fih: some schools may allow such testimony if the witness is trustworthy, but the Hanafi position is clear.
The Hanafi school adheres to the literal prohibition based on this hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والولد لأبويه : لقوله علي: "لا تقبل شهادة الولد لوالده، ولا الوالد لولده، ولا المرأة لزوجها، ولا الزوج لامرأته، ولا العبد لسيده، ولا المولى لعبده، ولا الأجير لمن استأجره"، ولأن المنافع بين هؤلاء متصلة فتكون شهادة لنفسه من وجه ولو قال والفرع لأصله وبالعكس، أو الولد لآبائه وأمهاته ،وبالعكس لكان أولى وأعم. [رمز الحقائق: ١٤٦/٢]
And the child for his parents: because of his (Ali's) saying: 'The testimony of a child for his father is not accepted, nor of a father for his child, nor of a woman for her husband, nor of a husband for his wife, nor of a slave for his master, nor of a master for his slave, nor of a hired worker for the one who hired him.' And because the benefits between these are connected, so the testimony would be for himself in a sense. And if he had said: the branch for the root and vice versa, or the child for his ancestors and mothers and vice versa, it would have been better and more general. [Rumor al-Haqaiq 146/2]
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (hadith) on the prohibition of testimony between close relatives due to conflict of interest. The matn phrase 'the child for his parents' is part of a longer ruling. The proof is a hadith attributed to Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) listing seven categories: child for parent, parent for child, wife for husband, husband for wife, slave for master, master for slave, and hired worker for the one who hired him. The reason given is that the benefits (manafi') between these individuals are connected (muttasilah), so testifying for one is like testifying for oneself in a sense. The commentary notes that if the wording had been 'the branch for the root' or 'the child for his ancestors and mothers' it would have been more comprehensive. This ruling is based on the principle of eliminating suspicion of partiality.

Key Terms
الولد لأبويه (al-walad li-abawayhi): A child testifying for his parents
المنافع متصلة (al-manafi' muttasilah): Mutual benefits that are interconnected, creating potential bias
الأجير (al-ajir): A hired worker

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A brother testifying for his brother
C) A husband testifying for his wife
D) A slave testifying for his master

Key Takeaway
Testimony between close relatives listed in the hadith is rejected due to interconnected benefits creating a conflict of interest.

Testimony of the Person Punished for Qadhf Even After Repentance
Ruling on rejecting testimony of one punished for qadhf with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on accepting the testimony of a person punished for qadhf after repentance, explain the grammatical argument based on istithna’ munqati‘, and compare the Hanafi view with the Shafi‘i position.

Core Idea
The testimony of a qadhf-punished person is permanently rejected even after repentance — the exception only applies to the description of fisq.

This lesson examines one of the most nuanced disputes between the Hanafi and Shafi‘i schools: does repentance restore the testimonial capacity of someone who has been flogged for falsely accusing another of zina? The ruling hinges on the interpretation of three verses in Surah al-Nur and the grammatical nature of the exception. We will first state the Hanafi position, then the opposing view, and finally analyze the proof structure that supports the Hanafi reliance.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What exactly does the Hanafi school hold regarding the testimony of a person punished for qadhf after repentance? Identify the ruling and any exceptions.
The exception is for a kafir who becomes Muslim after being punished.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school(s) disagree with the Hanafi position? What is their ruling?
Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and al-Batti accept testimony after repentance.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What dalil does the opposing school cite? Identify the Quranic verse and the principle they apply.
They cite Qur'an 2:160 'except those who repent' and use the rule that the excepted takes a ruling contrary to the excepted-from.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the opponent's argument? Focus on the grammatical analysis of the exception in Qur'an 24:4-5.
The Hanafi response uses the concept of istithna' munqati' (disconnected exception), arguing that the exception refers to the description of fisq, not to the prohibition of testimony.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in rulings between the two positions?
Hanafi: permanent rejection even after repentance. Shafi'i: acceptance after genuine repentance.

(عيني، فتح) والمحدود في قذف إن لم تقم بينة على صدقه وكل فاسق تاب عن فسقه قبلت توبته وشهادته إلا اثنين: المحدود في قذف والمعروف بالكذب، فتوبتهما مقبولة عند الله تعالى، لكن لا تقبل شهادتهما، وقال الشافعي: تقبل شهادة المحدود في قذف إذا تاب؛ لقوله تعالى: إِلَّا الَّذِينَ تَابُوالله (البقرة: ١٦٠)؛ إذ حكم المستثنى بخلاف المستثنى منه، وبه قال مالك وعثمان البتي ولنا: قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقْبَلُوا لَهُمْ شَهَادَةً أَبَدا (النور:٤)، والمستثنى صفة الفسق في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْفَاسِقُونَ (النور:٤)، والواو فيه للاستئناف لا للعطف، فيكون منقطعاً عن الأولى فينصرف الاستثناء إليه ضرورة. [رمز الحقائق: ١٤٥/٢] وأولئك أن هم منه فإن قيل: كيف ينصرف الاستثناء إلى ما يليه والأصل في الاستثناء هو المتصل، وهو أن يكون المستثنى من جنس المستثنى منه، والتوبة ليست من جنس الفسق، قلنا: الاستثناء من عموم الأحوال كأنه قال: - والله أعلم الفاسقون في جميع الأحوال إلا في حال التوبة، فيكون الاستثناء متصلا، أو نقول: الاستثناء منقطع بمعنى 'لكن'، كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّهُمْ عَدُوٌّ لِي إِلَّا رَبَّ الْعَالَمِينَ ﴾ (الشعراء: ۷۷) فلا . يرد السؤال. واعلم الضمير في 'لهم' من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلا تَقْبَلُوا لَهُمْ شَهَادَةً أَبَدا (النور:٤) عائد إلى المحدودين، وعند الشافعي إلى القاذفين العاجزين عن الإثبات، فلو لم يحد تقبل شهادته عندنا خلافاً له، ولو أقام أربعة بعد ما حد على أنه زنى تقبل شهادته بعد التوبة في الصحيح؛ لأنه بعد إقامة البينة لا يحد، فكذا لا ترد شهادته، وفي المحدود في غير القذف تقبل شهادته إن تاب. (فتح) وإن تاب: أي عن جناية القذف وهذا عندنا. (معدن) إلا أن يحد الكافر إلخ تقبل شهادته بعد الإسلام؛ لأن للكافر شهادة على مثله، له ذلك ومن وحد في القذف، كان رد شهادته من تتمة حد وبالإسلام حدثت له شهادة مطلقة غير الأولى، فلا يكون الرد من تمامها، بخلاف العبد إذا حد ثم أعتق حيث لم تقبل شهادته؛ لأنه لم يكن له شهادة على أحد وقت الجلد فلم يتم الرد إلا بعد الإعتاق، ولو ضرب الذمي في حد القذف سوطاً، ثم أسلم، ثم ضرب الباقي بعد الإسلام تقبل شهادته وعن أبي حنيفة إذا ضرب السوط الأخير بعد الإسلام، لا تقبل، وعنه: أنه إذا ضرب الأكثر بعد الإسلام لا تقبل شهادته، وإن كان دون ذلك تقبل؛ لأن للأكثر حكم الكل. رمز الحقائق: ١٤٦/٢] (عناية)
And the one punished for qadhf (false accusation of zina), even if he repents, except if a disbeliever is punished for qadhf then becomes Muslim, and the child for his parents, meaning for his father and mother against a Muslim, and based on this the testimony of the spouse is not accepted after it is rejected, even after divorce, so what is meant by after divorce is the completion of the 'iddah, and during the 'iddah it is not accepted. (al-'Ayni, al-Fath) And the one punished for qadhf, if he does not establish proof of his truthfulness, and every sinner who repents from his sin, his repentance and testimony are accepted except two: the one punished for qadhf and one known for lying; their repentance is accepted by Allah Almighty, but their testimony is not accepted. Al-Shafi'i said: The testimony of the one punished for qadhf is accepted if he repents, due to His statement: 'Except those who repent' (al-Baqarah 2:160), since the excepted has a ruling contrary to the excepted-from, and Malik and 'Uthman al-Batti agreed. And we have the statement of Allah: 'And do not ever accept from them testimony' (al-Nur 24:4), and the excepted is the description of fisq in His statement: 'And those are the defiantly disobedient' (al-Nur 24:4), and the waw is for beginning a new sentence, not for coordinating conjunction, so it is disconnected from the preceding, so the exception necessarily refers to it. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:145] 'And those... they are from him' If it is said: How does the exception refer to what follows it, when the origin in exception is the connected type, which is that the excepted is of the same genus as the excepted-from, and repentance is not of the genus of fisq? We say: The exception is from the general circumstances, as if He said: 'And Allah knows best, the defiantly disobedient in all circumstances except in the state of repentance', so the exception is connected. Or we say: The exception is disconnected meaning 'but', like His statement: 'Indeed, they are an enemy to me, except the Lord of the worlds' (al-Shu'ara' 26:77), so the question does not arise. Know that the pronoun 'them' in His statement 'And do not ever accept from them testimony' (al-Nur 24:4) refers to those punished for qadhf, and according to al-Shafi'i it refers to those who accuse [but] are unable to prove. Thus if one is not flogged, his testimony is accepted according to us, contrary to him. And if one brings four witnesses after being flogged that he committed zina, his testimony is accepted after repentance in the correct view, because after establishing proof he is not flogged, so likewise his testimony is not rejected. And concerning one punished for something other than qadhf, his testimony is accepted if he repents. (al-Fath) 'And if he repents': i.e., from the crime of qadhf, and this is according to us. (al-Ma'din) 'Except if a disbeliever is punished... etc.' His testimony is accepted after Islam, because a disbeliever has testimony against his like — he has that. And the one punished for qadhf, the rejection of his testimony is part of the completion of the hadd punishment, and with Islam a new absolute testimony arises for him, not the first one, so the rejection is not part of its completion, unlike a slave who was flogged then freed, where his testimony is not accepted, because he had no testimony against anyone at the time of flogging, so the rejection was not completed except after emancipation. And if a dhimmi is struck one whip in the hadd of qadhf, then becomes Muslim, then the remaining whip is struck after Islam, his testimony is accepted. According to Abu Hanifa: if the last whip is struck after Islam, it is not accepted. And according to him: if the majority is struck after Islam, his testimony is not accepted; if less than that, it is accepted, because the majority has the ruling of the whole. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:146] (al-'Inayah)
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the ruling of accepting the testimony of a person who has been punished (hadd) for false accusation of zina (qadhf). The Hanafi position is that even after repentance, his testimony is rejected permanently. The exception is a kafir who was punished for qadhf then embraces Islam—his testimony becomes accepted. Al-Shafi'i holds that repentance restores testimonial capacity, citing the verse 'except those who repent'. Hanafis respond with the verse 'do not ever accept from them testimony' and argue that the exception in the following verse refers to the description of fisq, not to the prohibition of acceptance, using principles of Arabic grammar (munqati' vs muttasil istithna). The passage also discusses subsidiary issues: (1) The application to spousal testimony after rejection, (2) The distinction between qadhf and other hadd punishments, (3) The case of a slave flogged then freed, (4) The scenario of a dhimmi flogged partially then accepting Islam. The relied upon position (mufta bihi) is that the testimony of a qadhf-punished person is never accepted after repentance, except if he repents by becoming Muslim from kufr, which creates a new capacity.

Key Terms
المحدود في قذف (al-mahdūd fī qadhf): A person who has been subjected to the hadd punishment for falsely accusing someone of zina.
توبة (tawbah): Repentance from sin; in this context, turning away from the false accusation and its sin.
استثناء منقطع (istithnā’ munqaṭi‘): A disconnected exception where the excepted item is not of the same genus as the excepted-from; here the statement 'except those who repent' is not connected to the preceding prohibition of testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the testimony of a person punished for qadhf not accepted even after repentance?
A) Because his repentance is not accepted by Allah.
B) Because the Qur'anic phrase 'except those who repent' refers to the description of fisq, not to the command rejecting testimony.
C) Because he must also give financial compensation.
D) Because rejection of testimony is part of the hadd punishment that cannot be removed by repentance.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school firmly rules that the testimony of a person punished for qadhf is never accepted after repentance, based on a grammatical interpretation of Qur'anic exceptions.

Testimony of Close Relatives: The Hanafi Rule of Tuhma
Listing categories of witnesses whose testimony is rejected due to relationship or mutual benefit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven categories of witnesses whose testimony is rejected due to close relationship or mutual benefit, and apply the Hanafi principle of tuhma to identify further categories and exceptions.

Core Idea
Testimony is rejected when there is suspicion of partiality (tuhma) due to close relationship or financial interest.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents the Hanafi ruling that testimony between certain relatives and associates is not accepted. The core proof is a hadith from Ali, and the underlying rationale is the suspicion of self-interest (tuhma). We will explore the explicit categories, the extensions by qiyas, the inter-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i on spousal testimony, and the general principle that any testimony bringing gain or repelling loss is rejected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven explicit categories
From the hadith of Ali: list the seven pairs where testimony is rejected: child for parent, parent for child, wife for husband, husband for wife, slave for master, master for slave, employee for employer.
Memorize these seven as the foundation.
2
Understand the underlying cause (tuhma)
The reason for rejection is the connected benefits (ittisal al-manafi') that create suspicion the witness is testifying for themselves indirectly.
Tuhma is the 'illa; other categories are added by qiyas when the same 'illa exists.
3
Trace the extensions by qiyas
The text extends the ruling to grandparents, parents-in-law, spouses during 'iddah, master for mukatab, partner for partner in partnership matters, and private employees. Each extension is justified by the presence of tuhma.
Note that the story of Hasan and Husayn testifying for Ali illustrates strict application even for the Prophet's grandsons.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i objection on spousal testimony
Al-Shafi'i accepts spousal testimony, arguing both are just individuals. The Hanafi response cites the hadith and the principle of tuhma: one spouse may favor the other.
This is a key point of ikhtilaf with practical implications.
5
Apply the general principle and its exceptions
The final maxim: 'every testimony that brings gain or repels loss is not accepted due to suspicion.' But exceptions exist where tuhma is absent, such as testimony by a public employee (ajir 'amm) like a tailor, or a partner testifying outside the partnership scope.
The distinction between ajir khas and ajir 'amm is critical.

Source Text

[رمز الحقائق: ١٤٦/٢] وجديه، وعكسه، وأحد الزوجين للآخر، والسيّد لعبده ومكاتبه، والشريك لشريكه أي في شيء أي ولا تقبل أيضاً شهادة الشريك في ما هو من شركتهما، والمخنث والنائحة . - وروي أن حسن بن علي هما شهد لعلي الله مع قنبر مولاه عند شريح بدرع له فقال شريح لعلي ه: ايت بشاهد آخر فقال: مكان الحسن أو مكان قنبر فقال: لا، بل مكان الحسن قال: أما سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول للحسن والحسين هما: 'هما سيدا شباب أهل الجنة في الجنة، قال: سمعت لكن ايت بشاهد آخر، أما سمعته يقول: 'لا تقبل شهادة الولد لوالده إلى آخر القصة فاستحسنه وزاده في الرزق. (فتح) وعكسه مرفوع على أنه مبتدأ وخبره محذوف أي عكس الحكم المذكور كالحكم المذكور، فلا تقبل شهادة أبويه وجديه للولد (عيني) وأحد الزوجين للآخر ولو في عدة ،الثلاث والمعتبر في المنع الزوجية عند القضاء لا عند التحمل والأداء، وفي باب الرجوع في الهبة الزوجية مانعة وقت الهبة، لا وقت الرجوع، وفي باب إقرار المريض تعتبر الزوجية وقت الإقرار، وفي باب الوصية تعتبر وقت الموت لا وقت الوصية، وأطلق في الزوجية، فشمل الأمة؛ لأن لها حقاً في المشهود به، وقال الشافعي: تقبل شهادة أحد الزوجين للآخر؛ لأنه عدل شهد للغير، ولنا ما رويناه (عيني فتح والسيد أي ولا تقبل شهادة السيد إلخ. (عيني) لعبده: سواء كان على العبد دين أو لم يكن (معدن) مكاتبه : أي ولا شهادة السيد لمكاتبه. (عيني) والشريك لشريكه وكذا لا تقبل شهادة الأجير الخاص لمستأجره مسالمة أو مشاهرة، أو التابع، أو التلميذ الخاص الذي يعد ضرر أستاذه ضرر نفسه ونفعه نفع نفسه، وهو معنى قوله علي: 'لا شهادة للقانع بأهل البيت' أي لطالب معاشه منهم، من القنوع بمعنى السؤال لا بمعنى القناعة والحاصل: أن كل شهادة جرت مغنماً أو دفعت مغرماً لم تقبل؛ للتهمة، وشهادة الشريك شهادة لنفسه من وجه، فلا تقبل، ولو شهد له فيما ليس من شركتهما تقبل كالأجير العام مثل الخياط، فإنها تقبل؛ لانتفاء التهمة. (عيني، فتح)
And the child for his parents: due to his statement (Ali): 'Do not accept the testimony of a child for his parent, nor a parent for his child, nor a woman for her husband, nor a husband for his wife, nor a slave for his master, nor a master for his slave, nor a hired worker for the one who hired him.' And because the benefits between these are connected, so it is testimony for himself in some respect. And if he said 'the descendant for his ancestor' and vice versa, or 'the child for his fathers and mothers' and vice versa, it would be more appropriate and comprehensive. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 2:146] And his grandparents, and its opposite, and one spouse for the other, and the master for his slave and mukatab, and the partner for his partner — i.e., in anything, meaning the testimony of a partner is also not accepted in what is part of their partnership, and the effeminate man and the wailing woman. And it is related that al-Hasan ibn 'Ali and [Husayn] testified for 'Ali, with Qanbar his mawla, before Shuraih regarding a shield of his. Shuraih said to 'Ali: 'Bring another witness.' He said: 'Instead of al-Hasan or instead of Qanbar?' He said: 'No, rather instead of al-Hasan.' He said: 'Did you not hear the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) say concerning al-Hasan and al-Husayn: 'They are the leaders of the youth of Paradise in Paradise'?' He said: 'I heard, but bring another witness. Did you not hear him say: 'Do not accept the testimony of a child for his parent' to the end of the story?' So he admired it and increased his provision. (al-Fath) And its opposite is marfu' as subject and its predicate omitted, i.e., the opposite of the mentioned ruling is like the mentioned ruling, so the testimony of his parents and grandparents for the child is not accepted. (al-'Ayni) And one spouse for the other, even during the 'iddah of three. And the consideration in prohibition is the marriage at the time of judgment, not at the time of bearing or giving testimony. And in the chapter on revocation of gift, marriage is a preventive factor at the time of gift, not at the time of revocation. And in the chapter of a sick person's confession, marriage is considered at the time of confession. And in the chapter of bequest, it is considered at the time of death, not at the time of bequest. And it is unrestricted in marriage, so it includes a slave woman because she has a right in the subject matter. Al-Shafi'i said: The testimony of one spouse for the other is accepted because he is a just person testifying for another. And we have what we narrated. (al-'Ayni, al-Fath) And the master: i.e., the testimony of the master is also not accepted for his slave, whether the slave has a debt or not. (al-Ma'din) His mukatab: i.e., the testimony of the master for his mukatab. (al-'Ayni) And the partner for his partner: likewise, the testimony of a private employee (ajir khas) for his employer (musta'jir) by the month or day, or a follower, or a private student who considers harm to his teacher as harm to himself and benefit to him as benefit to himself, and this is the meaning of his statement (Ali): 'No testimony for the one who is content (qani') with the people of the house' i.e., one who seeks his livelihood from them, from al-qanu' meaning asking, not from contentment. The conclusion: every testimony that brings gain (maghnam) or repels loss (maghram) is not accepted due to suspicion. And the testimony of a partner is testimony for himself in some respect, so it is not accepted. But if he testifies for him in what is not part of their partnership, it is accepted, like a public employee such as a tailor, for his testimony is accepted due to the absence of suspicion. (al-'Ayni, al-Fath)
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof listing categories of witnesses whose testimony is rejected due to close relationship or conflict of interest. It begins with the hadith narrated from 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) listing seven categories: child for parent, parent for child, wife for husband, husband for wife, slave for master, master for slave, and hired worker for employer. The underlying reason is the connection of benefits (ittisal al-manafi'), making the testimony in one's own interest. The text expands this rule to include grandparents, parents-in-law, spouses even during 'iddah, master for slave and mukatab, and partner for partner in partnership matters. A famous story is related about al-Hasan and al-Husayn testifying for 'Ali before Qadi Shuraih, who rejected their testimony based on the hadith. This illustrates the strict application of the rule even for the Prophet's grandsons. The passage also discusses the timing of the marriage relationship for the prohibition, noting differences with al-Shafi'i on spousal testimony. The general principle is established: any testimony that brings gain or repels loss is not accepted due to suspicion (tuhma). The exception is for public employees (ajir 'amm) or testimony in matters outside the partnership, where no suspicion exists. The relied-upon Hanafi position is that these testimonies are not accepted, though al-Shafi'i accepts spousal testimony.

Key Terms
التهمة (al-tuhma): Suspicion of partiality due to a relationship or interest that could influence the witness to lie or favor.
الأجير الخاص (al-ajīr al-khāṣṣ): A private employee hired exclusively by one employer (by month or day), whose testimony for that employer is rejected due to tuhma.
القانع بأهل البيت (al-qāni‘ bi-ahli al-bayt): One who seeks his livelihood from the people of a household, meaning dependent on them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the underlying cause (illa) for rejecting testimony in the seven categories listed in the hadith?
A) The witnesses are not just ('adl).
B) Suspicion of partiality (tuhma) due to connected benefits.
C) The testimony is about one's own case.
D) The Quran explicitly forbids such testimony.

Key Takeaway
Testimony is rejected when there is suspicion of partiality due to close relationship or mutual benefit; the Hanafi school lists specific categories and extends them by qiyas to any situation where tuhma is present.

Shahadat — Testimony

Testimony Rejected Due to Tuhma (Suspicion of Bias)
Defines whose testimony is rejected due to personal relationship and tuhma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the categories of witnesses whose testimony is rejected due to tuhma and apply the principle to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Testimony is rejected when relationship or dependence creates suspicion, even if the witness is truthful.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq enumerates specific relationships that disqualify a witness: grandparents, children, spouses, masters over slaves, and business partners. The underlying principle is tuhma (suspicion of bias) — not actual dishonesty. The proof is the incident where even Hasan and Husain were not allowed to testify for Ali. We will dissect each category and understand the rule's scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed categories and the overarching principle
Read the matn: jaddayhi (grandparents), aksuhu (child for parent), spouses, master for slave/mukatab, partner for partner. Note the commentator adds specific hireling, follower, and student. The principle is any testimony that brings gain or repels loss to the witness is rejected due to tuhma.
Tuhma is the key legal concept.
2
Examine the proof: the incident of Hasan and Husain
The text relates that Hasan and Husain testified for Ali against Qanbar. Shuraih rejected the testimony because of the father-son relationship, citing the hadith 'La tuqbal shahadat al-walad li waladihi'. This establishes the rule even for the most upright witnesses.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but used as evidence.
3
Analyze the temporal rule: when does the relationship matter?
The commentator clarifies that the relevant time is the time of judgment (al-qada), not the time of bearing or giving testimony. This applies to spouses: if they are divorced before judgment, testimony may be accepted after iddah. Similarly for gifts, confessions, and wills, different rules apply.
Pay attention to the specific context for each transaction.
4
Understand the madhhab dispute on spousal testimony
Al-Shafi'i accepts spousal testimony because the spouse is adl and testifies for another. Hanafi response: the marital relationship creates tuhma that overcomes the adl. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.
The dispute is legitimate; both sides have proofs.
5
Apply the principle to unlisted cases: specific hireling, follower, student
The text extends the rule to those economically dependent: the ajir al-khas (monthly/yearly employee) cannot testify for his employer; a student who considers his teacher's harm as his own likewise. The general hireling (e.g., tailor) can testify because no special dependence.
The principle is not limited to the listed six categories.

Source Text
